        <title>The wild sweetpotato ( Ipomoea trifida ) genome provides insights into storage root development | BMC Plant Biology | Full Text</title>

        

    <meta name="citation_abstract" content="Sweetpotato (Ipomoea batatas (L.) Lam.) is the seventh most important crop in the world and is mainly cultivated for its underground storage root (SR). The genetic studies of this species have been hindered by a lack of high-quality reference sequence due to its complex genome structure. Diploid Ipomoea trifida is the closest relative and putative progenitor of sweetpotato, which is considered a model species for sweetpotato, including genetic, cytological, and physiological analyses. Here, we generated the chromosome-scale genome sequence of SR-forming diploid I. trifida var. Y22 with high heterozygosity (2.20%). Although the chromosome-based synteny analysis revealed that the I. trifida shared conserved karyotype with Ipomoea nil after the separation, I. trifida had a much smaller genome than I. nil due to more efficient eliminations of LTR-retrotransposons and lack of species-specific amplification bursts of LTR-RTs. A comparison with four non-SR-forming species showed that the evolution of the beta-amylase gene family may be related to SR formation. We further investigated the relationship of the key gene BMY11 (with identity 47.12% to beta-amylase 1) with this important agronomic trait by both gene expression profiling and quantitative trait locus (QTL) mapping. And combining SR morphology and structure, gene expression profiling and qPCR results, we deduced that the products of the activity of BMY11 in splitting starch granules and be recycled to synthesize larger granules, contributing to starch accumulation and SR swelling. Moreover, we found the expression pattern of BMY11, sporamin proteins and the key genes involved in carbohydrate metabolism and stele lignification were similar to that of sweetpotato during the SR development. We constructed the high-quality genome reference of the highly heterozygous I. trifida through a combined approach and this genome enables a better resolution of the genomics feature and genome evolutions of this species. Sweetpotato SR development genes can be identified in I. trifida and these genes perform similar functions and patterns, showed that the diploid I. trifida var. Y22 with typical SR could be considered an ideal model for the studies of sweetpotato SR development."/>

    <link rel="canonical" href="//bmcplantbiol.biomedcentral.com/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z"/>
    

        
        
        
        <meta property="og:url" content="https://bmcplantbiol.biomedcentral.com/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z"/>
        <meta property="og:type" content="article"/>
        <meta property="og:site_name" content="BMC Plant Biology"/>
        <meta property="og:title" content="The wild sweetpotato ( Ipomoea trifida ) genome provides insights into storage root development"/>
        <meta property="og:description" content="Sweetpotato (Ipomoea batatas (L.) Lam.) is the seventh most important crop in the world and is mainly cultivated for its underground storage root (SR). The genetic studies of this species have been hindered by a lack of high-quality reference sequence due to its complex genome structure. Diploid Ipomoea trifida is the closest relative and putative progenitor of sweetpotato, which is considered a model species for sweetpotato, including genetic, cytological, and physiological analyses. Here, we generated the chromosome-scale genome sequence of SR-forming diploid I. trifida var. Y22 with high heterozygosity (2.20%). Although the chromosome-based synteny analysis revealed that the I. trifida shared conserved karyotype with Ipomoea nil after the separation, I. trifida had a much smaller genome than I. nil due to more efficient eliminations of LTR-retrotransposons and lack of species-specific amplification bursts of LTR-RTs. A comparison with four non-SR-forming species showed that the evolution of the beta-amylase gene family may be related to SR formation. We further investigated the relationship of the key gene BMY11 (with identity 47.12% to beta-amylase 1) with this important agronomic trait by both gene expression profiling and quantitative trait locus (QTL) mapping. And combining SR morphology and structure, gene expression profiling and qPCR results, we deduced that the products of the activity of BMY11 in splitting starch granules and be recycled to synthesize larger granules, contributing to starch accumulation and SR swelling. Moreover, we found the expression pattern of BMY11, sporamin proteins and the key genes involved in carbohydrate metabolism and stele lignification were similar to that of sweetpotato during the SR development. We constructed the high-quality genome reference of the highly heterozygous I. trifida through a combined approach and this genome enables a better resolution of the genomics feature and genome evolutions of this species. Sweetpotato SR development genes can be identified in I. trifida and these genes perform similar functions and patterns, showed that the diploid I. trifida var. Y22 with typical SR could be considered an ideal model for the studies of sweetpotato SR development."/>
        <meta property="og:image" content="https://media.springernature.com/w200/springer-static/cover/journal/12870.jpg"/>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Background</h3><p>Sweetpotato (<i>Ipomoea batatas</i> (L.) Lam.) is the seventh most important crop in the world and is mainly cultivated for its underground storage root (SR). The genetic studies of this species have been hindered by a lack of high-quality reference sequence due to its complex genome structure. Diploid <i>Ipomoea trifida</i> is the closest relative and putative progenitor of sweetpotato, which is considered a model species for sweetpotato, including genetic, cytological, and physiological analyses.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Results</h3><p>Here, we generated the chromosome-scale genome sequence of SR-forming diploid <i>I. trifida</i> var. Y22 with high heterozygosity (2.20%). Although the chromosome-based synteny analysis revealed that the <i>I. trifida</i> shared conserved karyotype with <i>Ipomoea nil</i> after the separation, <i>I. trifida</i> had a much smaller genome than <i>I. nil</i> due to more efficient eliminations of LTR-retrotransposons and lack of species-specific amplification bursts of LTR-RTs. A comparison with four non-SR-forming species showed that the evolution of the beta-amylase gene family may be related to SR formation. We further investigated the relationship of the key gene <i>BMY11</i> (with identity 47.12% to <i>beta-amylase 1</i>) with this important agronomic trait by both gene expression profiling and quantitative trait locus (QTL) mapping. And combining SR morphology and structure, gene expression profiling and qPCR results, we deduced that the products of the activity of <i>BMY11</i> in splitting starch granules and be recycled to synthesize larger granules, contributing to starch accumulation and SR swelling. Moreover, we found the expression pattern of <i>BMY11</i>, sporamin proteins and the key genes involved in carbohydrate metabolism and stele lignification were similar to that of sweetpotato during the SR development.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Conclusions</h3><p>We constructed the high-quality genome reference of the highly heterozygous <i>I. trifida</i> through a combined approach and this genome enables a better resolution of the genomics feature and genome evolutions of this species. Sweetpotato SR development genes can be identified in <i>I. trifida</i> and these genes perform similar functions and patterns, showed that the diploid <i>I. trifida</i> var. Y22 with typical SR could be considered an ideal model for the studies of sweetpotato SR development.</p></div></div></section>
                    
    


                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Background"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Background</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Sweetpotato (<i>Ipomoea batatas</i> (L.) Lam.), which is mainly cultivated for its underground storage root (SR), was found in the Americas approximately 800010,000years ago and domesticated at least 4000years ago [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Woolfe JA. Sweetpotato: an untapped food resource. Cambridge: Cambridge University Press; 1992." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d56259e957">1</a>]. This plant was then spread around the world, which could be traced back to the beginning of pre-Columbian times [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Roullier C, Benoit L, McKey DB, Lebot V. Historical collections reveal patterns of diffusion of sweet potato in Oceania obscured by modern plant movements and recombination. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2013;110(6):220510." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d56259e960">2</a>]. Because it is easy to grow, with high yield and an abundance of starch and nutrients, it has become an important part of diets around the world. In recent years, the global production of sweetpotato was more than 100 million tons (<a href="http://www.fao.org/faostat/en/#home">http://www.fao.org/faostat/en/#home</a>); it has become one of the three major root and tuber crops and the seventh most important food crop in the world [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Potato Genome Sequencing Consortium. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature. 2011;475(7355):18995." href="#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d56259e970">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wang H, Yang J, Zhang M, Fan W, Firon N, Pattanaik S, Yuan L, Zhang P. Altered Phenylpropanoid metabolism in the maize Lc-expressed sweet potato (Ipomoea batatas) affects storage root development. Sci Rep. 2016;6:18645." href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d56259e970_1">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Ceballos H, Kawuki RS, Gracen VE, Yencho GC, Hershey CH. Conventional breeding, marker-assisted selection, genomic selection and inbreeding in clonally propagated crops: a case study for cassava. Theor Appl Genet. 2015;128(9):164767." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d56259e974">5</a>]. Understanding the mechanism of SR formation and development is of pivotal importance for further improving sweetpotato yield [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Wang Z, Fang B, Chen X, Liao M, Chen J, Zhang X, Huang L, Luo Z, Yao Z, Li Y. Temporal patterns of gene expression associated with tuberous root formation and development in sweetpotato (Ipomoea batatas). BMC Plant Biol. 2015;15:180." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d56259e977">6</a>]. To date, sporamin proteins, Dof-type zinc finger proteins, MADS-box proteins and KNOX proteins have been shown to be associated with SR development [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Tanaka M. Recent progress in molecular studies on storage root formation in Sweetpotato (Ipomoea batatas). Jpn Agric Res Q. 2016;50(4):2939." href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d56259e980">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yeh KW, Chen JC, Lin MI, Chen YM, Lin CY. Functional activity of sporamin from sweet potato (Ipomoea batatas Lam.): a tuber storage protein with trypsin inhibitory activity. Plant Mol Biol. 1997;33(3):56570." href="#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d56259e980_1">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Yang S, Liu X, Qiao S, Tan W, Li M, Feng J, Zhang C, Kang X, Huang T, Zhu Y, et al. Starch content differences between two sweet potato accessions are associated with specific changes in gene expression. Funct Integr Genomics. 2018;18(6):61325." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d56259e983">9</a>]. The lack of genomic information has slowed research into SR development [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Tanaka M. Recent progress in molecular studies on storage root formation in Sweetpotato (Ipomoea batatas). Jpn Agric Res Q. 2016;50(4):2939." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d56259e986">7</a>]. The candidate genes corresponding to many sweetpotato quantitative trait loci (QTL) remain elusive [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Li H, Zhao N, Yu X, Liu Y, Zhai H, He S, Li Q, Ma D, Liu Q. Identification of QTLs for storage root yield in sweetpotato. Sci Hortic. 2014;170:1828." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d56259e989">10</a>]. The newly released haplotype sweetpotato genome [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Yang J, Moeinzadeh MH, Kuhl H, Helmuth J, Xiao P, Haas S, Liu G, Zheng J, Sun Z, Fan W, et al. Haplotype-resolved sweet potato genome traces back its hexaploidization history. Nat Plants. 2017;3(9):696703." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d56259e993">11</a>] provides an additional resource to help reach this goal, but the chromosome-scale assembly was performed according to the <i>Ipomoea nil</i> genome, which might not be suitable for genomics research, such as QTL investigations. Sweetpotato has a large number of small chromosomes (2<i>n</i>=6<i>x</i>=90, B<sub>1</sub>B<sub>1</sub>B<sub>2</sub>B<sub>2</sub>B<sub>2</sub>B<sub>2</sub>), with highly repetitive elements and high heterozygosity, and it contains a homogenous B<sub>2</sub>subgenome [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Yang J, Moeinzadeh MH, Kuhl H, Helmuth J, Xiao P, Haas S, Liu G, Zheng J, Sun Z, Fan W, et al. Haplotype-resolved sweet potato genome traces back its hexaploidization history. Nat Plants. 2017;3(9):696703." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1020">11</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Si Z, Du B, Huo J, He S, Liu Q, Zhai H. A genome-wide BAC-end sequence survey provides first insights into sweetpotato (Ipomoea batatas (L.) Lam.) genome composition. BMC Genomics. 2016;17(1):945." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1024">12</a>], which causes difficulty in generating high-quality sequences at the chromosome level [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Isobe S, Shirasawa K, Hirakawa H. Challenges to genome sequence dissection in sweetpotato. Breed Sci. 2017;67(1):3540." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1027">13</a>].</p><p>Among approximately 700 species of the genus <i>Ipomoea</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Padma Nimmakayala GV, Reddy UK. Charpetr 7: ipomoea. In: Kole C, editor. Wild crop relatives: genomic and breeding resources. Dordrecht: Springer Heidelberg; New York: Springer; 2011. p. 123." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1036">14</a>], <i>I. trifida</i> is the closest wild relative of sweetpotato [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Si Z, Du B, Huo J, He S, Liu Q, Zhai H. A genome-wide BAC-end sequence survey provides first insights into sweetpotato (Ipomoea batatas (L.) Lam.) genome composition. BMC Genomics. 2016;17(1):945." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1042">12</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Kriegner A, Cervantes JC, Burg K, Mwanga RO, Zhang D. A genetic linkage map of sweetpotato [Ipomoea batatas (L.) lam.] based on AFLP markers. Mol Breed. 2003;11(3):16985." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1045">15</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Roullier C, Duputie A, Wennekes P, Benoit L, Fernandez Bringas VM, Rossel G, Tay D, McKey D, Lebot V. Disentangling the origins of cultivated sweet potato (Ipomoea batatas (L.) Lam.). PloS One. 2013;8(5):e62707." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1049">16</a>]. Artificial crossing and cytogenetic research suggest that hexaploid <i>I. trifida</i> arose from diploid <i>I. leucantha</i> and tetraploid <i>I. littoralis</i>, while tetraploid <i>I. littoralis</i> was an autopolyploid from the <i>I. leucantha</i> B genome [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Nishiyama I. Evolution and domestication of the sweetpotato. Bot Mag Tokyo. 1971;84:37787." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1068">17</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Nishiyama I, Miyazaki T, Sakamoto S. Evolutionary autoploidy in the sweetpotato (Ipomoea batatas (L.) lam) and its progenitors. Euphytica. 1975;24(1975):197208." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1071">18</a>]<i>. I. leucantha</i> and <i>I. littoralis</i> should be considered the diploid and tetraploid forms of <i>I. trifida</i> based on interspecific hybridization and cytogenetics, respectively [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Nishiyama I, Miyazaki T, Sakamoto S. Evolutionary autoploidy in the sweetpotato (Ipomoea batatas (L.) lam) and its progenitors. Euphytica. 1975;24(1975):197208." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1083">18</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Shiotani I, Kawase T. Genomic structure of the sweet potato and hexaploid in Ipomoea trifida (H. B. K.) Don. Japan J Breed. 1989;39:5766." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1087">19</a>]. The artificial hexaploid <i>I. trifida</i> produced from diploid and tetraploid <i>I. trifida</i> has the same chromosome types as sweetpotato, and thus, sweetpotato may derive from hexaploid <i>I. trifida</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Nishiyama I, Miyazaki T, Sakamoto S. Evolutionary autoploidy in the sweetpotato (Ipomoea batatas (L.) lam) and its progenitors. Euphytica. 1975;24(1975):197208." href="#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1099">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Shiotani I, Kawase T. Genomic structure of the sweet potato and hexaploid in Ipomoea trifida (H. B. K.) Don. Japan J Breed. 1989;39:5766." href="#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1099_1">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Lu S, Liu Q, Li W. Sweetpotato breeding. Beijing: Chinese Agricultural Press; 1998." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1102">20</a>]. Moreover, data from noncoding chloroplast and nuclear ITS sequences and nuclear SSRs supported an autopolyploid origin of sweetpotato from a progenitor that shared the diploid <i>I. trifida</i> genome [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Roullier C, Duputie A, Wennekes P, Benoit L, Fernandez Bringas VM, Rossel G, Tay D, McKey D, Lebot V. Disentangling the origins of cultivated sweet potato (Ipomoea batatas (L.) Lam.). PloS One. 2013;8(5):e62707." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1109">16</a>]. Triploid <i>I. trifida</i> may have provided a bridge from diploid and tetraploid to hexaploid <i>I. trifida</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Roullier C, Duputie A, Wennekes P, Benoit L, Fernandez Bringas VM, Rossel G, Tay D, McKey D, Lebot V. Disentangling the origins of cultivated sweet potato (Ipomoea batatas (L.) Lam.). PloS One. 2013;8(5):e62707." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1118">16</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Shiotani I, Kawase T. Genomic structure of the sweet potato and hexaploid in Ipomoea trifida (H. B. K.) Don. Japan J Breed. 1989;39:5766." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1121">19</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Lu S, Liu Q, Li W. Sweetpotato breeding. Beijing: Chinese Agricultural Press; 1998." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1125">20</a>]. Our recent result of restriction-site-associated DNA sequencing (RAD-seq) indicated that sweetpotato originated from hexaploid <i>I. trifida</i>, and that hexaploid <i>I. trifida</i> evolved from tetraploid <i>I. trifida</i> and diploid <i>I. trifida</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Feng JY, Li M, Zhao S, Zhang C, Yang ST, Qiao S, Tan WF, Qu HJ, Wang DY, Pu ZG. Analysis of evolution and genetic diversity of sweetpotato and its related different polyploidy wild species I. trifida using RAD-seq. BMC Plant Biol. 2018;18(1):181." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1140">21</a>]. However, <i>Wx</i> intron variations support an allohexaploid origin of sweetpotato from <i>I. tenuissima</i> and tetraploid <i>I. littoralis</i> Blume or tetraploid <i>I. tabascana</i>, and the <i>I. tenuissima</i> derived earlier from diploid <i>I. trifida</i> and an unidentified species [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Gao M, Ashu GM, Stewart L, Akwe WA, Njiti V, Barnes S. Wx intron variations support an allohexaploid origin of the sweetpotato [Ipomoea batatas (L.) lam]. Euphytica. 2011;177(1):11133." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1163">22</a>]. Besides, the results of the newly released haplotype-resolved sweetpotato genome also suggested that sweetpotato may the result of an initial cross between a tetraploid progenitor and a diploid <i>I. trifida</i> progenitor [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Yang J, Moeinzadeh MH, Kuhl H, Helmuth J, Xiao P, Haas S, Liu G, Zheng J, Sun Z, Fan W, et al. Haplotype-resolved sweet potato genome traces back its hexaploidization history. Nat Plants. 2017;3(9):696703." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1169">11</a>]. Either it is the most likely progenitor of sweetpotato or a part of its genome has introgressed into that of sweetpotato [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Munoz-Rodriguez P, Carruthers T, Wood JRI, Williams BRM, Weitemier K, Kronmiller B, Ellis D, Anglin NL, Longway L, Harris SA, et al. Reconciling conflicting phylogenies in the origin of sweet potato and dispersal to Polynesia. Curr Biol. 2018;28(8):12461256.e1212." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1172">23</a>]. In any case, diploid <i>I. trifida</i> is at a critical point in the complex evolutionary history of sweetpotato, and the origin of sweetpotato remains disputed. Therefore, many studies have focused on diploid <i>I. trifida</i>; a genome survey has been reported [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Hirakawa H, Okada Y, Tabuchi H, Shirasawa K, Watanabe A, Tsuruoka H, Minami C, Nakayama S, Sasamoto S, Kohara M, et al. Survey of genome sequences in a wild sweet potato, Ipomoea trifida (H. B. K.) G. Don. DNA Res. 2015;22(2):1719." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1182">24</a>], and another genome assembly can be viewed online (<a href="http://sweetpotato.plantbiology.msu.edu/new.shtml">http://sweetpotato.plantbiology.msu.edu/new.shtml</a>). Previous studies have mainly focused on the fibrous root (FR), pencil root (PR) or thick root (TR) of <i>I. trifida</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Lu S, Liu Q, Li W. Sweetpotato breeding. Beijing: Chinese Agricultural Press; 1998." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1195">20</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hirakawa H, Okada Y, Tabuchi H, Shirasawa K, Watanabe A, Tsuruoka H, Minami C, Nakayama S, Sasamoto S, Kohara M, et al. Survey of genome sequences in a wild sweet potato, Ipomoea trifida (H. B. K.) G. Don. DNA Res. 2015;22(2):1719." href="#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1198">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Komaki K, Katayama K. Root thickness of diploid Ipomoea trifida (H. B. K.) G. Don and performance of progeny derived from the cross with sweetpotato. Breed Sci. 1999;49:1239." href="#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1198_1">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Austin DF. The Ipomoea batatas complex-I. Taxonomy. Bull Torrey Bot Club. 1978;105(2):11429." href="#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1198_2">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Eserman LA, Jarret RL, Leebens-Mack JH. Parallel evolution of storage roots in morning glories (Convolvulaceae). BMC Plant Biol. 2018;18(1):95." href="#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1198_3">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Wu S, Lau KH, Cao Q, Hamilton JP, Sun H, Zhou C, Eserman L, Gemenet DC, Olukolu BA, Wang H, et al. Genome sequences of two diploid wild relatives of cultivated sweetpotato reveal targets for genetic improvement. Nat Commun. 2018;9(1):4580." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1201">28</a>]. Few studies have reported on the SR, possibly because of a lack of diploid material with SR, and use of the genome to study SR development has not yet been reported.</p><p>Here, we report a high-quality, chromosome-anchored reference genome of the diploid <i>I. trifida</i> var. Y22 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Feng JY, Li M, Zhao S, Zhang C, Yang ST, Qiao S, Tan WF, Qu HJ, Wang DY, Pu ZG. Analysis of evolution and genetic diversity of sweetpotato and its related different polyploidy wild species I. trifida using RAD-seq. BMC Plant Biol. 2018;18(1):181." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1210">21</a>], which has typical SR and is similar to sweetpotato (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S1). We de novo assembled the highly heterozygous genome (2.20%) with a combined strategy, and 30,227 gene models were predicted. We found that a whole-genome triplication (WGT) occurred before its speciation, approximately 74.5 million years ago (MYA). Through an integrated analysis of gene family evolution, root comparative transcriptomes, QTL mapping, qPCR, we found that the new gene <i>BMY11</i> (with identity 47.12% to <i>beta-amylase 1</i>) may play a key role in the process of SR development. The key genes between Y22 and sweetpotato have a similar expression pattern, and sweetpotato SR development genes can be identified by QTL mapping of the diploid <i>I. trifida</i> genetic population. This work will be very helpful to further understand the complex evolutionary history and SR development mechanisms of <i>I. trifida</i> and sweetpotato.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome assembly, validation and annotation</h3><p>We constructed a 180bp paired-end (PE) library and sequenced the Y22 genome using Illumina PE125, which yielded 62.27 Gb of data for genome survey analysis. K-mer frequency [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Li R, Zhu H, Ruan J, Qian W, Fang X, Shi Z, Li Y, Li S, Shan G, Kristiansen K, et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 2010;20(2):26572." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1241">29</a>] distribution analysis showed that the genome of Y22 was 476.6Mb, consistent with the estimate based on flow cytometry [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Li A, Wu ZM, Zhou ZL, Zhang A, Ma DF, Li YD, Zhao DL, Tang J, Cao QH. The construction of genomic Fosmid library and library PCR screening system on Ipomoea trifida( Kunth) G.Don. Acta Agriculturae Boreali-Sinica. 2014;29(2):4550." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1244">30</a>], and the heterozygosity and the proportion of repeat sequences were 2.20 and 48.42%, respectively (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S1, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM1">1</a>: Figures S2 and S3). To achieve a high-quality and chromosome-scale reference genome, 125.6 Gb of clean Illumina shotgun reads (approximately 264-fold coverage of the genome) and 537Mb of Illumina Moleculo synthetic long reads (approximately 1-fold coverage of the genome) (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S2) were initially assembled into 431.57Mb of sequence with a contig N50 of 26.50 Kb and a scaffold N50 of 580.68 Kb (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S3). Then, we incorporated 10.05 Gb (21-fold coverage of the assembly) of PacBio RS II data with an N50 read length of 17.03 Kb to increase sequence continuity. The contig and scaffold N50 of the final assembly were 54.49 Kb and 607.92 Kb, respectively (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S4). Additionally, 96.71% of the <i>I. trifida</i> genome was covered by the assembled 460.93Mb scaffolds. A genotype-by-sequencing (GBS) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Zhou Z, Zhang C, Zhou Y, Hao Z, Wang Z, Zeng X, Di H, Li M, Zhang D, Yong H, et al. Genetic dissection of maize plant architecture with an ultra-high density bin map based on recombinant inbred lines. BMC Genomics. 2016;17:178." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1266">31</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Zhang Z, Wei T, Zhong Y, Li X, Huang J. Construction of a high-density genetic map of Ziziphus jujuba Mill. using genotyping by sequencing technology. Tree Genet Genomes. 2016;12(4):76." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1269">32</a>] genetic map was constructed using 200 true F1 individuals (Y25Y22) (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM1">1</a>: Figures S1 and S4; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM2">2</a>: Tables S5 and S6), and 400.44Mb of sequence (86.88% of the final scaffolds) was successfully anchored to 15 chromosomes (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM1">1</a>: Figures S5 and S6), which could be considered as a better reference for <i>I. trifida</i> than the recent released genome (373.4Mb scaffolds were anchored to 15 linkage groups) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Wu S, Lau KH, Cao Q, Hamilton JP, Sun H, Zhou C, Eserman L, Gemenet DC, Olukolu BA, Wang H, et al. Genome sequences of two diploid wild relatives of cultivated sweetpotato reveal targets for genetic improvement. Nat Commun. 2018;9(1):4580." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1285">28</a>].</p><p>The quality and completeness of the assembly were evaluated by various datasets. First, we mapped the short insert size library reads to the assembled genome; the mapping rate was 95.2, and 95.76% of the assembled genome had more than 20-fold coverage (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S7). The GC content and sequence depth distribution calculated for the 10k non-overlapping sliding window of the assembled genome showed that the assembled genome was not contaminated (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM1">1</a>: Figures S7 and S8). Second, the full-length transcripts assembled from 7.2 Gb of leaf RNA-seq reads by Trinity [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Grabherr MG, Haas BJ, Yassour M, Levin JZ, Thompson DA, Amit I, Adiconis X, Fan L, Raychowdhury R, Zeng Q, et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nat Biotechnol. 2011;29(7):64452." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1297">33</a>] were aligned back to the assembled genome. The results showed that more than 98.80% of the full-length transcript sequences could be mapped back onto one scaffold with sequence coverage more than 50% (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S8), which was comparable to the results of the highly heterozygous assembly [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Zhang G, Fang X, Guo X, Li L, Luo R, Xu F, Yang P, Zhang L, Wang X, Qi H, et al. The oyster genome reveals stress adaptation and complexity of shell formation. Nature. 2012;490(7418):4954." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1303">34</a>]. Third, 98.39% of 248 core eukaryotic genes (Cluster of Essential Genes (CEG) database) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Parra G, Bradnam K, Korf I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics. 2007;23(9):10617." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1307">35</a>] could be aligned back to the genome assembly with high confidence (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S9). And the 93.9% of the BUSCO [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Simao FA, Waterhouse RM, Ioannidis P, Kriventseva EV, Zdobnov EM. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics. 2015;31(19):32102." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1313">36</a>] conserved genes were complete in the assembly. These results indicated that our assembled genome achieved complete coverage of the conserved genes.</p><p>In total, 30,227 gene models were predicted in the assembled genome of <i>I. trifida</i> and 79.76% of these genes were supported by expression evidence (RNA-seq reads) from seven different tissues including leaf, flower, stigma, pollen, stem, root and seed (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S10). Additionally, 84.75% of all the gene models had homology hits with &gt;50% high-scoring segment pair coverage in the sequences of seven species, including <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Beta vulgaris</i>, <i>Capsicum annuum</i>, <i>Sesamum indicum</i>, <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i>, <i>Solanum tuberosum</i> and <i>Vitis vinifera</i> (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S9). A total of 28,456 genes (94.14% of all genes) were annotated based on homology to known proteins from the Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes (KEGG), Swiss-Prot, TrEMBL and Gene Ontology (GO) public databases (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S11). We identified the candidate noncoding RNA (ncRNA) sequences for <i>I. trifida</i> by comparison with known ncRNA libraries and by structural prediction, and the ratio of miRNA and tRNA in the genome were identified as 0.0310 and 0.0423%, respectively (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S12). There were 50.86% repeat sequences in the genome, including 5.92% tandem repeats. Long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons were the most abundant elements, comprising 30.41% of the genome, whereas DNA transposons, long interspersed nuclear elements (LINEs), and short interspersed nuclear elements (SINEs) accounted for 13.15, 4.86 and 0.68% of the genome, respectively (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S13). We mapped the distributions of genes, GC contents, repetitive sequences, <i>Gypsy</i>, <i>Copia</i>, and DNA repeats of the <i>I. trifida</i> genome to obtain an overview of the genome characterization (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#Fig1">1</a>). We found that the transposable elements are primarily located within chromosome pericentromeric regions, while most genes are located on the chromosome arms.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12870-019-1708-z/MediaObjects/12870_2019_1708_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12870-019-1708-z/MediaObjects/12870_2019_1708_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="680" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Genome characterization of diploid <i>I. trifida</i>. <b>a</b> Gene density per Mb. <b>b</b> GC content per Mb. <b>c</b> Repeat content per Mb. <b>d</b> <i>Gypsy</i> content per Mb. <b>e</b> <i>Copia</i> content per Mb. <b>f</b> DNA repeat content per Mb. <b>g</b> The syntenic regions between different chromosomes were identified by MCScanX. Syntenic regions larger than 350 Kb were shown. Each chromosome is assigned a color, and the color of the links between chromosomes is determined by the color of chromosome which has a smaller number in the pair</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Comparative genomics analysis</h3><p>Phylogenetic inference from eight plant genomes (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S14) using 1930 single-copy gene families illustrated that <i>I. trifida</i> and <i>I. nil</i> have a common ancestor, which separated from a common ancestor of <i>S. tuberosum</i> and <i>Ipomoea</i> spp. approximately 78.8 MYA and <i>I. trifida</i> and <i>I. nil</i> diverged from their common ancestor around 6.4 MYA (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#Fig2">2</a>a). Despite their close relationship, the estimated genome size of <i>I. trifida</i> was much smaller than that of <i>I. nil</i> (476.6Mb vs 750Mb) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Hoshino A, Jayakumar V, Nitasaka E, Toyoda A, Noguchi H, Itoh T, Shin IT, Minakuchi Y, Koda Y, Nagano AJ, et al. Genome sequence and analysis of the Japanese morning glory Ipomoea nil. Nat Commun. 2016;7:13295." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1464">37</a>]. A comparison of the repeat contents of these two Convolvulaceae genomes showed that the <i>I. nil</i> genome contains a higher abundance of repetitive elements, especially the LTR retrotransposon family, than that of <i>I. trifida</i> does (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S15). The solo LTR / intact LTR ratio of <i>I. trifida</i> (0.96:1) is notably higher than that of <i>I. nil</i> (0.38:1), indicating higher recombination frequencies in the <i>I. trifida</i> genome, which may eliminate transposable elements (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S16). Furthermore, we identified collinear genome regions between <i>I. trifida</i> and <i>I. nil</i> based on the gene blocks detected by MCScanX [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Wang Y, Tang H, Debarry JD, Tan X, Li J, Wang X, Lee TH, Jin H, Marler B, Guo H, et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 2012;40(7):e49." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1496">38</a>]. The overall size of the syntenic blocks in the <i>I. trifida</i> genome is 248.3Mb, which is smaller than that in the <i>I. nil</i> genome (339.9Mb). In the syntenic blocks, the total length of the repetitive sequences of <i>I. trifida</i> is 97.1Mb, less than that of <i>I. nil</i> (165.0Mb). The repeat sequences occupied 39.1% of these syntenic blocks in <i>I. trifida</i>, a smaller ratio than that of <i>I. nil</i> (48.5%) (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S17). These results suggest that repetitive elements are the major factors contributing to the difference in genome size between <i>I. trifida</i> and <i>I. nil</i>, probably due to the species-specific amplification bursts of LTR-RTs in <i>I. nil</i> (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S10) and more efficient eliminations of LTR-retrotransposons in <i>I. trifida</i>. Although the transposable elements of <i>I. nil</i> are more prevalent than those of <i>I. trifida</i>, the two species share the same chromosome number, indicating that no large-scale chromosome fission or fusion occurred after their speciation (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S11). The newly released haplotype-resolved sweetpotato assembly has similar chromosome synteny with <i>I. trifida</i> (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S12), which is as expected, because the sweetpotato pseudochromosomes were generated according to gene and sequence synteny between sweetpotato and <i>I. nil</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Yang J, Moeinzadeh MH, Kuhl H, Helmuth J, Xiao P, Haas S, Liu G, Zheng J, Sun Z, Fan W, et al. Haplotype-resolved sweet potato genome traces back its hexaploidization history. Nat Plants. 2017;3(9):696703." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1556">11</a>]. Although Yang et al. has developed a novel haplotyping method to efficiently solve the assembly problem for polyploidy genomes, the monoploid assembly has a lower scaffold N50 (~201 Kb) due to its high polymorphism level and limited sequencing depth (~67-fold based on hexaploid genome size). Hence, more detailed comparisons between <i>I. trifida</i> and sweetpotato should be carried out once a more high-quality chromosome reference of the sweetpotato genome is obtained with the integration of long-read sequencing technology, Hi-C sequencing and assembly algorithms developed for polyploid genomes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Zhang J, Zhang X, Tang H, Zhang Q, Hua X, Ma X, Zhu F, Jones T, Zhu X, Bowers J, et al. Allele-defined genome of the autopolyploid sugarcane Saccharum spontaneum L. Nat Genet. 2018;50(11):156573." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1563">39</a>].</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12870-019-1708-z/MediaObjects/12870_2019_1708_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12870-019-1708-z/MediaObjects/12870_2019_1708_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="537" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Evolution of the <i>I. trifida</i> genome and gene families. <b>a</b> Phylogenetic tree showing the divergence times of eight species. The blue dots represent WGD events, and the red dots represent WGT events. The grey circles indicate the genome sizes of the eight species. <b>b</b> Distribution of 4dTv values of syntenic genes. The dotted lines represent comparisons of each species with its own sequences, and peaks indicate genome duplication/triplication events. The solid peaks indicate divergence events between species. Itr: <i>I. trifida</i>, Stu: <i>S. tuberosum</i>, Cca: <i>C. canephora</i>, Ini: <i>I. nil</i>. <b>c</b> The large circle shows <i>I. trifida</i> - <i>I. trifida</i> intragenomic syntenic regions putatively derived from WGT, which were detected by a series of paralogous genes (only chromosomes 4, 9, 13 and 15 are shown). The bottom circle shows collinear blocks between chromosome 1 of <i>C. canephora</i> and chromosomes 6, 7 and 11 of <i>I. trifida</i>. The right circle shows collinear blocks between chromosome 4 of <i>C. canephora</i> and chromosomes 5, 12 and 13 of <i>I. trifida</i>. The colour of each link corresponds to the colour of the chromosome. <b>d</b> The expansion of gene families associated with SR in <i>I. trifida</i></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Whole-genome duplication (WGD) events in <i>I. trifida</i> were investigated using paralogous regions detected by protein sequence similarity to reveal their importance in genome evolution [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Van de Peer Y, Maere S, Meyer A. The evolutionary significance of ancient genome duplications. Nat Rev Genet. 2009;10(10):72532." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1639">40</a>]. We identified 1856 intra diploid <i>I. trifida</i> syntenic blocks (with at least 5 genes per block), which contained 17,630 genes, accounting for ~58.33% of the total genes. A four-fold transversion (4dTv) analysis indicated that the WGD occurred at the peak around 0.31, approximately 74.5 MYA (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#Fig2">2</a>a, b) which is similar to the reported result [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Hoshino A, Jayakumar V, Nitasaka E, Toyoda A, Noguchi H, Itoh T, Shin IT, Minakuchi Y, Koda Y, Nagano AJ, et al. Genome sequence and analysis of the Japanese morning glory Ipomoea nil. Nat Commun. 2016;7:13295." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1648">37</a>]. Notably, these WGD/WGT events of this historical period may have enabled the survival of the most recent common ancestor of these species across the Cretaceous-Paleogene boundary (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#Fig2">2</a>a), as attested in many other angiosperms [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Vanneste K, Maere S, Van de Peer Y. Tangled up in two: a burst of genome duplications at the end of the Cretaceous and the consequences for plant evolution. Philos Trans R Soc Lond Ser B Biol Sci. 2014;369(1648). &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1098/rstb.2013.0353&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1655">41</a>]. A gene block comparison of <i>I. trifida</i> versus itself also showed numerous duplicated gene pairs (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#Fig1">1</a>), which is clear structural evidence of a WGT event<i>.</i> We found that chromosomes 4, 9, 13 and 15 of <i>I. trifida</i> each obviously have two additional paralogous segments from other chromosomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#Fig2">2</a>c). This result indicated that a WGT event occurred in the <i>Ipomoea</i> genome instead of the reported WGD [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Hoshino A, Jayakumar V, Nitasaka E, Toyoda A, Noguchi H, Itoh T, Shin IT, Minakuchi Y, Koda Y, Nagano AJ, et al. Genome sequence and analysis of the Japanese morning glory Ipomoea nil. Nat Commun. 2016;7:13295." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1677">37</a>]. To confirm this WGT event, we further compared the genome of <i>I. trifida</i> with that of <i>Coffea canephora</i>, which has no lineage-specific WGD/WGT event except the older <i>gamma</i> genome triplication in the common ancestor of most eudicots [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Denoeud F, Carretero-Paulet L, Dereeper A, Droc G, Guyot R, Pietrella M, Zheng C, Alberti A, Anthony F, Aprea G, et al. The coffee genome provides insight into the convergent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis. Science. 2014;345(6201):11814." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1690">42</a>]. As expected, the structural analysis showed that one <i>C. canephora</i> region tends to correspond to three segments of <i>I. trifida.</i> More specifically, chromosome 1 of <i>C. canephora</i> has a syntenic relationship with chromosomes 7, 9 and 11 of <i>I. trifida</i>, and chromosome 4 of <i>C. canephora</i> has a syntenic relationship with chromosomes 5, 12 and 13 of <i>I. trifida</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#Fig2">2</a>c)<i>.</i> Furthermore, the mutual coverage of <i>C. canephora</i> and <i>I. trifida</i> achieved the maximal value under a corresponding relationship of 1:3. Similarly, comparing <i>I. trifida</i> with <i>Vitis vinifera</i>, the genome coverage reached a maximal value of 93.2% for <i>I. trifida</i> and of 95.8% for <i>V. vinifera</i> at the ratio of 3:1, which also supports the results of the above WGT analysis (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S18).</p><p>The expanded and contracted gene families of <i>I. trifida</i> were identified using CAFE [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="De Bie T, Cristianini N, Demuth JP, Hahn MW. CAFE: a computational tool for the study of gene family evolution. Bioinformatics. 2006;22(10):126971." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1746">43</a>] among <i>I. trifida</i> and four non-SR-forming species, including <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>S. lycopersicum</i>, <i>C. canephora</i> and <i>I. nil.</i> There were 910 expanded gene families enriched in 22 pathways (<i>p</i> value&lt;0.05). Notably, some of these families have been reported to have functions in carbohydrate metabolism, for example, glycosaminoglycan degradation, carbon metabolism, and carbon fixation in photosynthetic organisms (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S19). Among these expanded gene families, we found that several of them may be important for SR development based on the functional annotation, such as <i>beta-amylase</i> genes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Potato Genome Sequencing Consortium. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature. 2011;475(7355):18995." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1774">3</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Monroe JD, Storm AR. Review: the Arabidopsis beta-amylase (BAM) gene family: diversity of form and function. Plant Sci. 2018;276:16370." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1778">44</a>], <i>MADS-box</i> genes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Ku AT, Huang YS, Wang YS, Ma D, Yeh KW. IbMADS1 (Ipomoea batatas MADS-box 1 gene) is involved in tuberous root initiation in sweet potato (Ipomoea batatas). Ann Bot. 2008;102(1):5767." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1784">45</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Noh SA, Lee HS, Huh EJ, Huh GH, Paek KH, Shin JS, Bae JM. SRD1 is involved in the auxin-mediated initial thickening growth of storage root by enhancing proliferation of metaxylem and cambium cells in sweetpotato (Ipomoea batatas). J Exp Bot. 2010;61(5):133749." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1787">46</a>], ethylene-responsive transcription factor (<i>ERF</i>) genes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Xu ZS, Chen M, Li LC, Ma YZ. Functions and application of the AP2/ERF transcription factor family in crop improvement. J Integr Plant Biol. 2011;53(7):57085." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1793">47</a>], <i>beta-galactosidase</i> genes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Goujon T, Minic Z, El Amrani A, Lerouxel O, Aletti E, Lapierre C, Joseleau JP, Jouanin L. AtBXL1, a novel higher plant (Arabidopsis thaliana) putative beta-xylosidase gene, is involved in secondary cell wall metabolism and plant development. Plant J. 2003;33(4):67790." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1800">48</a>], IAA-related genes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Chapman EJ, Estelle M. Mechanism of auxin-regulated gene expression in plants. Annu Rev Genet. 2009;43:26585." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1803">49</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Sha Luo QL, Liu S, Pinas NM, Tian H, Wang S. Constitutive expression of OsIAA9 affects starch granules accumulation and root gravitropic response in arabidopsis. Front Plant Sci. 2015;6:1156." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1806">50</a>] and gibberellic acid (GA)-related genes such as <i>GA20ox</i> and gibberellin-regulated protein [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Wang GL, Xiong F, Que F, Xu ZS, Wang F, Xiong AS. Morphological characteristics, anatomical structure, and gene expression: novel insights into gibberellin biosynthesis and perception during carrot growth and development. Hortic Res. 2015;2:15028." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1812">51</a>] (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#Fig2">2</a>d). Besides, there were 100 contracted gene families enriched in 15 KEGG pathways, such as plant-pathogen interaction, ABC transporters, and ascorbate and aldarate metabolism (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S20).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Identification of key genes in SR development</h3><p>We tested the root diameters and starch contents of four typical stages in <i>I. trifida</i> SR development. The starch content of adventitious root (AR, S0) was 0.0042%; it rapidly increased to 17.54% in initiating storage root (ISR, S1) and reached 43.93% in mature storage root (MSR, S3) (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#Fig3">3</a>a). The root diameter increased from that of AR to more than 20mm (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S1). These results indicated that <i>I. trifida</i> SR development is strongly associated with starch accumulation. We thus compared gene expression using the RNA-seq data (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S21) from the S0, S1, S2 and S3 samples to identify key genes in SR development (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S13a). Comparing to S0, we identified 211, 718, and 791 upregulated genes in S1, S2 and S3 respectively (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S13c). Notably, the starch and sucrose metabolism KEGG pathway ranked respectively the top enrichment for the three upregulated gene sets (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S22). We found that several key genes involved in starch metabolism, including <i>beta-amylase</i>, <i>AGPase</i> (ADP glucose pyrophosphorylase), <i>SSS</i> (soluble starch synthase), <i>SBE</i> (starch branching enzyme) and <i>GBSS</i> (granule bound starch synthase), were upregulated in one or two groups among S1, S2 and S3 compared with S0 (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S14). And more, there are 109 common upregulated genes during SR development (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S13c). Among these genes, eight genes that belong to starch metabolism pathways [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Nakkanong K, Yang JH, Zhang MF. Starch accumulation and starch related genes expression in novel inter-specific inbred squash line and their parents during fruit development. Sci Hortic. 2012;136:18." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1877">52</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="McGregor CE, He L, Ali RM, Sosinski B, Jankowicz J, Burg K, LaBonte DR. The effect of replicate number and image analysis method on sweetpotato [Ipomoea batatas (L.) Lam.] cDNA microarray results. Plant Mol. Biol. Rep. 2005;23: 367-381." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1880">53</a>], including <i>GPT</i> (Glucose-6-phosphate translocator) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="McGregor CE, He L, Ali RM, Sosinski B, Jankowicz J, Burg K, LaBonte DR. The effect of replicate number and image analysis method on sweetpotato [Ipomoea batatas (L.) Lam.] cDNA microarray results. Plant Mol. Biol. Rep. 2005;23: 367-381." href="#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1887">53</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kharabian-Masouleh A, Waters DL, Reinke RF, Ward R, Henry RJ. SNP in starch biosynthesis genes associated with nutritional and functional properties of rice. Sci Rep. 2012;2:557." href="#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1887_1">54</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Dyson BC, Allwood JW, Feil R, Xu Y, Miller M, Bowsher CG, Goodacre R, Lunn JE, Johnson GN. Acclimation of metabolism to light in Arabidopsis thaliana: the glucose 6-phosphate/phosphate translocator GPT2 directs metabolic acclimation. Plant Cell Environ. 2015;38(7):140417." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1890">55</a>], <i>PGM</i> (Phosphoglucomutase), <i>ISA</i> (Isoamylase), <i>SP</i> (starch phosphorylase), <i>DPEP</i> (4-alpha-glucanotransferase) and 3 <i>Beta-amylase</i> genes (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S14). <i>KNOX</i> and <i>MIKC-like</i> (type II <i>MADS-box</i>) genes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Ku AT, Huang YS, Wang YS, Ma D, Yeh KW. IbMADS1 (Ipomoea batatas MADS-box 1 gene) is involved in tuberous root initiation in sweet potato (Ipomoea batatas). Ann Bot. 2008;102(1):5767." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1922">45</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Noh SA, Lee HS, Huh EJ, Huh GH, Paek KH, Shin JS, Bae JM. SRD1 is involved in the auxin-mediated initial thickening growth of storage root by enhancing proliferation of metaxylem and cambium cells in sweetpotato (Ipomoea batatas). J Exp Bot. 2010;61(5):133749." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1925">46</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Tanaka M, Kato N, Nakayama H, Nakatani M, Takahata Y. Expression of class I knotted1-like homeobox genes in the storage roots of sweetpotato (Ipomoea batatas). J Plant Physiol. 2008;165(16):172635." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1928">56</a>] were also identified among the 109 genes. Notably, among these 109 genes, we also found that two specific protein sporamin were highly expressed (RPKM=32,145~113,739) (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S15, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S23). Sporamin is the major storage protein and accounts for 60 to 80% of the total soluble protein in the sweetpotato SR, and its expression has been shown to be primarily associated with SR [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Senthilkumar R, Yeh KW. Multiple biological functions of sporamin related to stress tolerance in sweet potato (Ipomoea batatas lam). Biotechnol Adv. 2012;30(6):130917." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1937">57</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Shewry PR. Tuber storage proteins. Ann Bot. 2003;91(7):75569." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1941">58</a>]. The sporamin highly expressed in <i>I. trifida</i> SR which indicated that the sweetpotato sporamin may inherit from <i>I. trifida.</i> Besides, KEGG enrichment of the differentially downregulated genes displayed that phenylalanine metabolism and phenylpropanoid biosynthesis were the top two pathways enriched in S1, S2 and S3 compared with S0 (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S13b); and we found that the key genes in lignin biosynthesis, <i>C4H</i> (coumarate 4-hydroxylase) and <i>F5H</i> (ferulate 5 -hydroxylase) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Shi R, Sun YH, Li Q, Heber S, Sederoff R, Chiang VL. Towards a systems approach for lignin biosynthesis in Populus trichocarpa: transcript abundance and specificity of the monolignol biosynthetic genes. Plant Cell Physiol. 2010;51(1):14463." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d56259e1960">59</a>], were downregulated from ARs swelling to ISRs, and kept lowly expression in the process of SRs enlargement (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S16, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S24). These results indicated that starch accumulation is negatively correlated with lignin formation, and it may be regulated by some metabolism pathways.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12870-019-1708-z/MediaObjects/12870_2019_1708_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12870-019-1708-z/MediaObjects/12870_2019_1708_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="559" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Identification of <i>BMY11</i>. <b>a</b> The graph above shows the starch contents of four typical stages of SR development. The image below shows the four typical stages (white arrow) of SR development. S0, adventitious root (AR); S1, initiating storage root (ISR); S2, young storage root (YSR); S3, mature storage root (MSR). Bar=2cm. <b>b</b> <i>BMY11</i> (<i>Itr.Sc0000035.86</i>) was located on chromosome 3. <b>c</b> Phylogenetic tree of the <i>beta-amylase</i> genes from five species. Different colours represent different species. The right side shows expression heatmaps of the 17 <i>beta-amylase</i> genes of <i>I. trifida</i>. * The heatmaps of genes with RPKM=0 were not shown due to invalid calculation</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To further investigate the key genes responding to SR starch accumulation, we used the starch contents of the dried roots of 200 true F1 individuals and two parents for QTL analysis with a GBS genetic map (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM1">1</a>: Figures S17 and S18). We obtained five QTLs, which were all located in chromosome 3, including 39 genes (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S25). Four of them were upregulated, and one was <i>BMY11</i> (Itr.Sc0000035.86, with identity 47.12% to <i>beta-amylase 1</i>, named <i>BMY11</i>) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#Fig3">3</a>b). Notably, among the four upregulated genes, <i>beta-amylase</i> gene was identified above among both the expanded gene families in the genome and the differentially upregulated genes. We examined all the members of this gene family in <i>I. trifida</i> and four other plants, i.e. <i>I. nil</i>, <i>S. lycopersicum</i>, <i>C. canephora</i>, and <i>A. thaliana</i>, and compared them. We found that the <i>beta-amylase</i> gene number in <i>I. trifida</i> was higher than that in non-SR-forming plants (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-019-1708-z#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S19).
